# Complete Guide to C Programming Foundations

> Do you want to learn how to program in the C language? In this course, creator, teacher, and technology writer Dan Gookin takes any individual interested in programming from not knowing how to code to being able to develop simple programs. This thorough examination of C touches on every topic relevant to a coder wanting to deep-dive into the language. Dan covers data storage, structures, pointers,

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations) | 5h 52m | Beginner | 12K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] At over 50 years old, C remains one of the top computer programming languages a required course at many universities.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=8)** C is often called the Latin of computer languages because when you know C, learning other languages is easier, yet, like learning, Latin, C can be a beast.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=18)** This course is a concept-based approach to introducing the skills necessary to program in the C language.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=24)** Each video focuses on a specific part of the language.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=27)** Basic processes are repeated as new concepts are introduced.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=31)** It's how I learned C, exploring the language and discovering it at the same time.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/diving-into-c?u=76281980&t=37)** I'm Dan Gookin, author of the book, C Programming for Dummies, and I invite you to join me and explore this Complete Guide to C Programming Foundations.

### 1. C Programming Fundamentals

> [↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Coding your first program
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here is the shortest C program.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=2)** you can write, please code along with me using this blank exercise file.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=7)** Type the word main, main is the primary function and starting point for all C programs and it's used in other programming languages as well.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=16)** Type an empty set of parentheses and then an empty set of braces or curly brackets.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=22)** That's it, this code is called the dummy.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=26)** It's the shortest C program and it does nothing but it's still a program.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=31)** Run the program and there's no output.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=36)** Nothing is shown here, the program does nothing, it's dumb.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=41)** A more traditional C program, the first one shown to most students is a little bit more involved and you can modify this code to be that program.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=49)** Start at the first line by typing a hash and then the word include and then an angle brackets type stdio.h in angle bracket and add a blank line.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=62)** This is the include pre-processor directive.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=65)** It tells the compiler to fetch the C code stored in the file, stdio.h the standard input output header file.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=73)** Then for the main function type int, int means that this function returns a whole number or integer value back to the operating system.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=84)** Main, as I said earlier, is the starting point for all programs, the main function.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=89)** This time however, the braces will not be empty.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=92)** They will contain statements that provide C language directions to the computer, put them on a new line and type puts PUTS parentheses, double quote, and then a string hello world.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=114)** The puts function or puts string function has a string argument in parentheses, all statements in C end with a semicolon.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=122)** It's like a period at the end of a sentence, but it's a semicolon, not a period.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=127)** Type a blank line and then a new statement, return zero.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=133)** The final statement in the main function is the return keyword, passing the main functions integer value zero back to the operating system.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=141)** Traditionally, zero means, okay, semicolon, ensure that that closing curly bracket is there to end the main function and run.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=154)** There's the text, hello world.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=157)** What you see here is a source code file.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=159)** It's plain text, but this isn't the program.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/coding-your-first-program?u=76281980&t=161)** These are just the directions to create the program.
>

#### Getting into the C language
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] The C programming language was developed in the 1970s, created by doctors Brian Kernighan and Dennis Ritchie, who were also involved with the creation of the Unix operating system.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=11)** It's based on the B language.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=13)** The B stood for Bell Labs, where both men worked.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=18)** C is considered a mid-level language.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=20)** It has features of both low-level, and high-level programming languages.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=24)** It's a procedural language, not object oriented.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=28)** And it was conceived of as a better way to write low-level machine code, which runs fast, but takes a long time to develop.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=35)** C was also written to be transferrable between many platforms.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/getting-into-the-c-language?u=76281980&t=40)** Here's the standard Hello World Program, shown in Assembly Code and in C.

#### Reviewing code structure
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Computer code has a certain look.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=2)** It ensures that your code is readable and understandable to other programmers.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=6)** C code runs top down.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=9)** The first line here is a comment.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=11)** It's just a note to myself or another programmer.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=14)** The comment is enclosed between slash asterisk and asterisk slash characters.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=19)** The text between these characters is ignored, skipped over, as if it wasn't there.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=24)** Next you see the include pre-processor directive.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=27)** It tells the compiler to take in the standard IO header file, stdio.h, and insert its contents here.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=36)** All C programs start with the main function.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=38)** The function has a type int for integer.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=41)** Its name is followed by parentheses in which arguments appear.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=45)** None are used here.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=47)** Functions contain statements which are enclosed in a set of curly brackets or braces.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=53)** Braces are also used within a function to collect a block of statements.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=58)** Here's a block between lines 11 and 15.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=62)** These statements belong to the for loop statement at line 10.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=67)** Braces block statements belonging to the loop, as well as statements belonging to decisions and other structures in C.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=74)** Lines six through 17 contain statements belonging to the main function.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=79)** A statement is a specific C language expression, like a command or sentence in English.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=85)** The expression can be an assignment, a mathematical operation, comparison, function call, and so on.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=92)** All statements in C end with a semicolon, which is required.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=96)** At line 10, the for statement lacks a semicolon because the block of statements serves the same purpose.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=103)** The if statement at line 13 is split between two lines for readability, its semicolon appears here at the end of line 14.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=112)** This construction is still considered a single statement in the C language.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=117)** Run the code so you can see what it does.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=123)** And I'll type in 100.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=126)** The code only generates 10 lines of output, which is a condition built into the program.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=137)** Here is a similar source code file, lines nine and 10 are the for loop statement, but instead of a block, both of these lines are considered a single statement.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=146)** Now, I could enclose the statement in braces, but it's not really necessary as only one statement is considered in the loop.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=158)** Still, this format is valid and the entire look here is very representative of a C source code file.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=164)** These items, the tabs, spaces, and blank lines are collectively referred to as white space.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=172)** The white space makes the code readable, but it's ignored by the compiler.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=176)** Its purpose is only for readability for your eyeballs.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=180)** In fact, I can remove all the white space and the code doesn't change.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=196)** The pre-processor directive must still be on a line by itself.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=200)** Text between double quotes is a string literal, which doesn't contain white space, but the main function and all its statements appear on line two minus all the white space.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=211)** Run and it still works.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=223)** Nothing has changed.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reviewing-code-structure?u=76281980&t=224)** My point here is that white space is optional, but as you can see, it definitely aids in code readability.

#### Working the C development cycle
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Creating a C language program requires three steps.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=3)** Write the source code, compile and link, test run the program.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=8)** You start by writing the source code. Use a text editor.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=12)** C language source code is plain text, no fancy fonts or layouts.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=17)** The code runs top down, just as you read text.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=20)** Save the source code file with the .c file name extension.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=27)** This is an example of a typical C source code skeleton file, often used to start new projects.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=33)** You see it here in a text editor, which is part of this integrated development environment or IDE.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=42)** The next step is to compile and link.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=45)** The compiler translates the source code into object code.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=48)** The linker combines the object code with a C library, which creates the final program.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=53)** In a modern IDE both compiling and linking are combined into a single process called build.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=62)** The compiler is what creates the object code file based on the C language source code.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=67)** Object code isn't a program, but rather tokens and shortcuts that help build the final program.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=74)** The object code file retains the same file name as the source code file, but with the .o file name extension.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=81)** The reason for this step is that a single program can be built from multiple object code files.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=88)** The linker takes the object code file and combines it with a C library.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=92)** It's these libraries that contain the routines that perform tasks for your specific computer or whatever target platform you're compiling for.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=100)** The result is a program.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-the-c-development-cycle?u=76281980&t=104)** In code spaces, click the run button here to build and run the program created.
>

#### Exploring the preprocessor
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C pre-processor helps prepare a source code file for compiling.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=4)** It uses pre-processor directives, instructions that are carried out before the source code is compiled to object code.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=11)** These instructions are not C code, but they help compile the source code and can provide some handy shortcuts.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=19)** While they traditionally appear at the start of a source code file, pre-processor directives can be used anywhere, providing they follow the proper format.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=27)** They can be disabled by enclosing them in a comment.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=32)** Here are the 12 pre-processor directives.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=35)** The most common are define and include.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=42)** A pre-processor directive starts with a hash or pound character at the start of a line.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=47)** This character is immediately followed by the directive.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=50)** Then comes a space and any options or arguments.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=53)** The directive ends with a new line, not a semicolon.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=58)** For the include pre-processor directive used here, the argument is a file name enclosed in angle brackets.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=65)** The include directive inserts the named file into the code at this spot.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=70)** The .h files are header files required by items in the source code.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=76)** In Linux and similar operating systems, header files are found in the usr/include or usr/local/include directories.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=84)** In windows, the include directories are relative to the compiler's directory.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=89)** When angle brackets are used with the include directive, the compiler looks in the default directories.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=95)** Otherwise, double quotes direct the pre-processor to search for the header file relative to the same directory as the source code file.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=105)** The defined directive is used for substitution.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=107)** Here at line five, the defined constant COUNT is created.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=111)** It references the value 10.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=113)** This defined constant, the text COUNT, is expanded by the compiler throughout the code wherever it appears.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=120)** At line 12, COUNT is replaced by 10 at compile time.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=124)** Defined constants are usually written in all caps, which makes them easy to locate.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=129)** The advantage here is that should you need to change the value in multiple locations in the source code file, you need only change the defined constant's value here once.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=141)** A string literal defined constant expression is enclosed in double quotes.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=145)** The double quotes are a part of the expression.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=148)** Here the defined constant greeting is expanded into the string Howdy do, which includes the quotes.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=153)** It's used in line seven in the puts() function to output text.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=159)** As a substitution directive, define can also create macros as shown in this code at line three.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=165)** The charout() macro is expanded to the putc() function shown here, both using argument A.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=172)** You see the macro appear in the code at line seven through 10 can run the code,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=187)** and that's the major output, Hi!.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=190)** The macro expands in the final program to output a series of characters.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-preprocessor?u=76281980&t=194)** These are just a few examples of how C pre-processor directives work in C code, which you'll see throughout this course.

#### Understanding header files and libraries
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's common for beginning C programmers to confuse header files with C language libraries.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=6)** These are two different things.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=8)** Header files are used in the source code and libraries are used by the linker.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=16)** Traditionally, the header files are found on a Unix system in the user include directory, and there they are.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=25)** When you use the include pre-processor directive with angle brackets, this is the primary location from which the header files are fetched.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=38)** The contents of these files are C language code.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=41)** You see definitions, prototypes, and pre-processor directives listed.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=48)** Library files are primarily located in the user lib directory.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=57)** And there they are.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=58)** When you create a C program, the linker looks in this directory for libraries to link in, such as the standard C library, which contains definitions for C functions such as printf.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=69)** These are object code files exactly like those created by the compiler that contain binary data, not C language code.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=77)** Other locations are used for header files and C language libraries as well, but these two are the traditional.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=85)** The point I'm driving at here is that header files are not the same as libraries.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=91)** Header files contain function definitions or prototypes as well as other tools to help you use C language functions.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=99)** The man page or documentation that describes the function also specifies which header file to include.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=106)** When you don't include a header file, the compiler generates a warning.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=112)** Man pages are accessed from the terminal window, though online copies are also available.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=117)** Type man and the C language function such as sqrt.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=122)** Here you see the manual page for the sqrt function, it requires the math.h header file.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=132)** Here's the function's formats.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=135)** There's also a note here requiring that you link in the math library, which is specific to this platform.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=142)** Type Q to quit the man program.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=147)** A library provides the muscle behind common C language functions.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=151)** In fact, these functions are called library functions for a reason.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=156)** A missing library or mistyped function name generates a linker error.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=160)** Remember that the man page explains which library to use for various functions.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=168)** This code includes the math.h header file.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=172)** It also contains a comment explaining how the header file fits into the big picture.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=177)** The sin or sine function at line 13 requires the math.h header file for its definition, as does the defined constant M_PI for the value pi.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=189)** But the gears that turn the wheel are found in the math library, which must be linked in when building the program.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-header-files-and-libraries?u=76281980&t=195)** You can't do that with a header file alone.

#### Adding comments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Comments are used for a variety of purposes.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=3)** They can provide information, documentation, and are used in debugging.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=7)** In the big picture, a comment is a part of your source code that the compiler ignores.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=12)** It skips over all the commented text.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=16)** The C language uses two styles of comment.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=19)** The traditional C language comment starts with the /* characters, and ends with the */ character.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=26)** All text between these character pairs is ignored by the compiler.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=31)** The C++ style comment starts with //.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=35)** All text after the two slashes is ignored until the end of the line.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=42)** This code shows both style of comments.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=45)** Lines 1 through 3 are a comment block using the traditional C language start and stop comment characters.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=51)** Line 4 contains a C++ style comment at the end of the line.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=57)** Line 9 has a comment at the end of the line as well, but it's enclosed in traditional C style comment characters.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=67)** One of the purposes of comments is to document your code.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=70)** The idea is to explain what you're doing to a future version of yourself, or a colleague who may someday work on your code.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=78)** You can use the comments to jot down notes, or to flag parts of the code for improvement later, as shown here at line 16.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=87)** A popular way to use comments is to disable part of the code for debugging purposes.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=92)** In this code, the statement at line 8 is disabled, it's been commented out.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=97)** And a note at line 5 explains perhaps how to fix the problem.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=103)** You can do just about anything with comments, save for two things.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=107)** First, you cannot nest traditional C style comments, putting one comment block inside another.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=113)** If so, the outer comment's terminating characters are interpreted as a warning or error, which may be highlighted in the editor, as shown here on line nine.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=125)** The second thing you can't do with comments is nest them inside a string literal, or inside any function or other part of the C language.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=133)** For a string, shown here at line 5, the comment characters become part of the string.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=138)** For a function or other misplaced comment, you see a compiler warning or a linker error, or perhaps it will be flagged by the editor.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=148)** Use comments to document your code, as useful notes to your future self, or another programmer, can come in handy.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=154)** Comments can disable code for debugging.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=157)** Avoid nested comments.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=159)** And remember that comments can work anywhere in your code, but not within a string literal, or inside a function, or other part of the C language.

> **Warnings:** warning (2)
> **Exercise Files:** source code (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** picture (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Understanding C language data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Programming is about input and output.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=3)** The old I/O you sang about at computer camp.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=6)** Input is received, modified, and then output.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=11)** It's data that's input, modified, and output.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=14)** The C language has four basic data types.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=17)** The data type describes what kind of information is manipulated in your code.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=25)** The four basic C data types are char, to store single characters or bytes of information, int to store integer values or whole numbers, float to store real numbers, these are decimals, fractions, and very large values in single precision, accurate to eight digits, and double to store real numbers at double precision, accurate to 16 digits.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=48)** These data types also have qualifiers which help refine the data type's scope.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=54)** For example, the signed qualifier is the default, meaning that an integer value stores both positive and negative values.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=62)** An unsigned qualifier means the integer stores only positive values.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=69)** This code prompts for four data types as input, then it outputs the values.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-c-language-data-types?u=76281980&t=74)** Four variables are declared at lines five through eight, each assigned a different data type and variable name, c, i, f, and d.
>

>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=4)** The variable must be declared before it's used.

>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=89)** Spaces are invalid in a variable name as is anything other than a letter, number, or the underscore.

>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=108)** Variable names can be any length, though only the first 31 characters of the name are significant.
>

>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=136)** This code shows how you can initialize a variable when it's declared, assigning it an immediate value as shown here at lines five, six, and seven.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=145)** All variables must be initialized before they're used, either when they're created, as shown here, or later in the code by grabbing user input or some other assignment.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=154)** Unlike other programming languages, C does not set an initial value for storage.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=160)** Variables are not initialized to zero.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=162)** Remember that.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=164)** Forgetting to assign values to variables before they're used is a source of frustration for many C programmers as well as a potential security risk.

#### Exploring the printf() function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C language most likely spawned the printf function, which is used in many other programming languages.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=6)** This function generates formatted output.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=9)** Its first or only argument is a formatting string as shown in this exercise file.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=14)** This text is output.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=16)** Unlike the puts function, a new line isn't added to this text automatically.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=21)** The new line escape character here does the job.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=28)** And there's the text.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=32)** The power in the printf function lies in the placeholders or conversion specifications found in the formatting string.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=39)** Here you see the percent D placeholder, corresponding to it is an additional argument, integer variable A.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=47)** These data types must match, percent D for decimal integer, and integer variable A.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=56)** Here is the formatted output.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=61)** This code's printf statement has three placeholders in its formatting string, percent C for character variable CH, percent U for unsigned integer variable X and percent F for floating point value pi.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=76)** Most editors color code the placeholders, that isn't happening here, but when it does, it really helps you find in the formatting string where they're located and ensure that they match the arguments presented.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=89)** Three placeholders here require three additional arguments and they must match data types.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=97)** Conversion specifications are prefixed by the percent character.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=101)** The percent is followed by optional characters to set output width, justification and other formatting details.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=108)** The conversion character itself comes last, which represents its companion arguments data type.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=115)** Each conversion character has a corresponding argument in the printf function, if not the compiler issues a warning, the conversion character and argument must match data types or a warning is generated.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=129)** Here are common conversion characters, D and I for decimal integer output, U for unsigned integers, F for floating point or real numbers and big E little E for scientific or E notation.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=145)** Big G little G outputs either E notation or decimal notation depending on the number's size.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=152)** C is for single characters.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=154)** S for strings, big X, little X for hexadecimal values with big or little letters in them.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=161)** O for integer output in octal or base eight, P for memory addresses in hexadecimal, Z for bytes or memory size values.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=171)** And finally, double percent sign to output a percent sign.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=177)** In this code you can see some the options used in a conversion specification.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=181)** The dot two in the percent F placeholder limits the floating point value's output to two digits after the decimal place.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=191)** And in the output you see only two digits from what is up here, a larger number.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=197)** I can edit this value, say a four, and see how it changes the output.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=205)** Now you see four digits after the decimal.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=208)** This is the power of the printf function.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-printf-function?u=76281980&t=213)** The full slate of options, and there are many, are available in the printf function's man page documentation.
>

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Constants are values that never change.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=3)** They can appear immediately as a literal value, or you can declare a constant expression.
>

> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=30)** This is good.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=33)** In this code, literal character constants appear inside a parade of putchar functions.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=44)** Hello.
>

>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=62)** Here you see a constant declaration at line five.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=65)** The const keyword classifies the integer identifier count as a constant value assigned four.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=73)** Creating a constant in this manner requires that it be initialized to a value just like this in a single statement.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=80)** The compiler issues an error if you attempt to change the value of a constant elsewhere in the code.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=86)** It's a constant, not a variable.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=92)** The advantage here is not only that the constant can't be altered accidentally, but that you can change its value as needed here on line five to affect all other locations where the constant is used in the code.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=105)** So I can change the value here to, let's see, 10.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=112)** And now run.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=116)** Changing the constant here saved me a lot of work plodding through the rest of the source code to make edits.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=124)** Like any variable, the const keyword creates a constant for use only within a function.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=130)** To create a constant for use in multiple functions in a source code file, use the define pre-processor directive.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=137)** Here at line three, the constant expression MAX is defined to represent the value 20 in all functions in this source code file.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=147)** It's used in the line function at line 12.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=153)** It also appears in the main function at line 23.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=157)** At both locations, the expression MAX is expanded to the value 20 by the pre-compiler.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=164)** Just like a regular constant, you can change the value of a defined constant in one spot to affect all instances throughout the source code file.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-constants?u=76281980&t=173)** This is an advantage of using constants in your code.

#### Understanding variable scope
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A storage class sets a C language variable's scope.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=4)** The scope defines the variable's availability to other functions in the source code file.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=11)** Three keywords define the classes.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=13)** An auto variable is local or private to the function in which it's defined.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=19)** The value is released after the function quits.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=22)** This is the default class.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=24)** The keyword auto is optional when declaring a variable.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=28)** Static variables are also local to their functions, but they retain their values after the function quits.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=36)** An external variable exists outside of, or external to, a function.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=41)** Its value is available to all functions.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=44)** This type of variable is also known as a global variable.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=50)** In this code, the variables A and B are local to the sum function.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=54)** Their values are used in this function then discarded when the function is done.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=63)** You can't see it in the output, but the values have been discarded when the function returned back to the main function.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=71)** The construction at line five is a bit odd because it uses the auto keyword, which is the default.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=78)** The compiler assumes that all variables are auto unless declared using another class, static or external.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=87)** The static keyword directs the program to retain a variable's value after a function ends.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=92)** In this code, function, myname, attempts to return character array, me, which is an auto or private function variable, by default.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=101)** The string should be displayed in the main function, but it won't because its variable is auto in scope and discarded when the function returns.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=112)** You may see some warnings regarding the variable's scope as the program compiles.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=117)** The warnings ring true.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=118)** Here it says The course's author is, and the word null has been substituted for what other nonsense would have appeared there, as the variable was discarded when the myname function quit.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=132)** To fix this problem, you can add the static keyword to the variable declaration in the myname function.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=141)** With this change, the variable's value will now be retained and seen in the output.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=149)** In this code, variables A and B are defined externally at line three.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=153)** You see that this statement exists outside of other functions in the source code file.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=158)** Within each function, the extern keyword declares variables A and B.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=164)** This keyword directs the compiler to look outside the function for variable declarations.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=168)** Variables A and B are used in both functions.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=172)** They are the same and have the same values.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=178)** While this approach works, better ways are available in C to share variables between functions.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=186)** It's tempting for C programmers and common in other languages to declare all variables external.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=192)** This practice works, but it's sloppy and inefficient.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=196)** External variables are required only when they must be accessed by all functions, or it's way too complicated to otherwise weave them into the code.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-variable-scope?u=76281980&t=204)** Please don't be a lazy C coder who uses external variables because you can't figure out another way to solve a specific programming puzzle.

> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1), is an  (1)

#### Making new data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you learn the C language, you discover various data types not among the clutch of C language keywords.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=6)** These are defined data types and they're also known as typedefs.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=14)** Here at line six, you see the time_t data type.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=17)** It declares the now variable.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=20)** As an experienced C programmer, I draw the conclusion that time_t is defined in a header file, most likely the time.h header file included at line two.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=31)** Further, see how the time_t data type is highlighted in the editor?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=35)** It must be legitimate, but if it's not a char, int, float or double, what is it?
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=41)** Specifically time_t is defined as a clock tick value, the number of seconds that have elapsed since Tuesday, January 1st, 1970.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=50)** This date is known as the Unix Epoch.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=53)** The time function obtains the system's current epoch value, saving it in time_t variable now.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=60)** The ctime function translates that value into a human readable string.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=65)** The printf function, swallowing the ctime function, outputs the results.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=73)** The output you see is current to whenever you run the program.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=78)** The time_t type is created by using the typedef keyword which defines new data types.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=85)** The typedef keyword is followed by a known data type and then the new definition.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=90)** For example, this statement defines time_t as a long unsigned integer.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=95)** You don't know for certain if this is the definition unless you examine the contents of the time.h header file where the definition occurs.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=104)** The reason for creating typedefs is that special data types may differ from system to system.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=111)** For example, time_t might be an unsigned short on some systems.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=115)** By using the typedef, the data type is consistent to maintain and compatible between systems.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=124)** In this exercise file, the typedef keyword creates a new data type, radius.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=128)** It's secretly a float as you can see here at line five, but in the code at line seven, radius variable a is declared.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=137)** The printf statement is split across several lines.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=140)** It outputs the value of radius variable a, then some math to represent pi R squared.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=151)** Here is the result.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=154)** A typedef can also be declared externally.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=156)** I'll move the line up here.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=163)** Run, and the output is the same.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=169)** These definitions are commonly held in a header file as shown earlier with the time.h header file and the time_t data type.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=179)** You also see typedef used with structures which helps reduce the structure declaration, which can be cumbersome.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=185)** Anytime you see a non-standard data type such as time_t, it was created by using a typedef statement.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/making-new-data-types?u=76281980&t=192)** Documentation in the code or on the functions man page should clue you in to how the defined data type is used, which printf placeholder to use and other important information.

#### Specifying integers and real numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Numbers in C come in two flavors.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=2)** The INT data type stores integers, or whole number values, the float and double data types store real numbers, are those that contain a decimal portion or are very large or very small.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=15)** Integer literals are written without commas as shown in line 5.

>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=204)** L, For long integer values, U, for unsigned integer values, F for float, though, you must add the decimal, the .0 part, or the compiler believes F to be an invalid character.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=216)** So the decimal requirement kind of makes the F redundant.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=220)** These letters can be upper or lower case, and you can pair up U and L for unsigned long integers.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/specifying-integers-and-real-numbers?u=76281980&t=227)** The L can also be used to suffix single character and string literals to specify wide or Unicode values.
