#### Typecasting a variable
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This code outputs the result of dividing two variables, A and B.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=4)** Both are integer variables.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=6)** The math lurks in the print F statement at line 10, A divided by B.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=12)** The placeholder for the resulting value is percent D, decimal integer output, which appears in the print F format string.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=22)** The program shows the result is 14, which isn't correct, but it's what you directed the program to do, divide two integers, so change the percent D placeholder to percent F, floating point output.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=39)** Let's see if this works.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=46)** And the answer is wrong again.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=50)** To truly get the desired result of the division operation, you must direct the compiler to work with the expression as a real number.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=58)** The operative term is typecast.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=61)** On line 10 before the math expression, type the word float in parentheses, now run.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=71)** Here is the proper answer.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=74)** This float thingy is a typecast.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=77)** It directs the compiler to treat the following expression as a real number, a float.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=82)** This expression now matches the percent F placeholder and division is done correctly.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=88)** Be aware that the typecast doesn't change the variable's underlying data type, it affects only whatever expression on which the cast is used, A divided by B in this case.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=101)** Typecasting acts like a mask that lets an expression pretend to be another data type.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=106)** In this code at line nine, the unsigned typecast is used on the time function to ensure that the value returned is always a positive integer.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=115)** If it were negative, the program might crash.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=118)** This typecast ensures that the value is always within expectations.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=123)** It's rare that you must typecast in your code.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=125)** My recommendation is to use the proper data type in the first place.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/typecasting-a-variable?u=76281980&t=129)** But when you need another type, in a pinch, you can typecast and prevent potential errors or coax the program into generating the desired result.

#### Chapter challenge: Adding variables and constants
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-adding-variables-and-constants?u=76281980)

>

> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=34)** This code shows the increment and decrement operators at lines 11 and 12 respectively.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=41)** The increment operator adds one to a value.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=44)** The decrement operator subtracts one.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=47)** These operators are the equivalent of these expressions, a = a + 1, and b = b - 1.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=57)** These expressions work because the math is done on the right first, then assigned to the value on the left.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=66)** But the increment and decrement operators offer a convenient shortcut.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=70)** These operators can be prefixed or postfixed to their variables.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=75)** Most often you see them postfixed, as shown here, but this position affects the math operation.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=82)** I'm going to move the statements into the first printf statement.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=85)** (keyboard clicking) Run to see the output.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=96)** You see that the increment and decrement operation takes place after the variable is used.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=102)** So one is added to a, and one is subtracted from b, which you see in the second line of output here.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=110)** If you want to increment our decrement before the variable is used, you prefix the operators.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=116)** (keyboard clicking) Now run to see the difference.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=125)** The operator affects the variable's value before it's used.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=130)** The modulus operator obtains the modulo of two values or the remainder of the first value divided by the second.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=138)** In this code, the modulus operator, the percent sign, is used at line 11.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=143)** The value of variable a is assigned the value of variable x modulus variable mod, which is input at line eight.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=153)** The printf statement outputs the results for values one through 10.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-math-operators?u=76281980&t=163)** I will try first with 2, and you see the remainder 0 for when two divides evenly, and 1, otherwise.

#### Solution: Do some math
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let me code a solution.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=6)** Variables A and C can be integers because the math operations don't involve divisions.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=14)** (keys tapping) Variable B however, must be a float because it's used in division.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=20)** (keys tapping continues) In the challenge source code file, I set a hint here in the second print F statement where you see this, the percent F placeholder.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=30)** This indicates a floating point value, and I hope you picked up on this hint.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=35)** Next come the variable assignments and all the math as outlined in the challenge.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=41)** (keys tapping) A is assigned the value 15 (keys tapping continues) B is assigned the value of variable A divided by 2, and I've used floating point notation for the literal here 0.0.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=55)** The 0.0 causes the compiler to interpret the value as a real number, (keys tapping) and variable C is assigned the value of variable B multiplied by 4, which again can be an integer.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=69)** I'll run my solution.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=73)** And the proper output shows A is 15, B is 7.5, and C is 30.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=79)** If you obtain these results, you pass the challenge, but if you saw only 7 as the result of variable B, it could be because you didn't specify 2.0, a real number as the denominator, or it could be that you tried to use an integer.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=92)** Always specify a float value for division, and use the decimal portion when assigning a value as a real number to ensure that the compiler yields the proper result.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-do-some-math?u=76281980&t=104)** I hope that you pass the challenge.

#### Using assignment operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In C programming, a variable can be modified by itself.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=4)** The format for self-modifying a variable is shown in this code.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=8)** Variable a is declared and initialized to 25 at Line 5.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=13)** Line 8 adds 5 to the value of variable a, Line 10 removes 5 from variable a, Line 12 multiplies the value of variable a by 5, and then Line 14 divides the value of variable a by 5.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=34)** These expressions work because, in C, the right side of the expression is evaluated first.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=39)** The answer then slides over through the equal sign into the variable on the left.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=43)** But there's a shortcut.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=46)** I'll modify the code to use assignment operators.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=51)** So a is increased by 5.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=57)** And a is decreased by 5.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=63)** A is multiplied by 5.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=67)** And a is divided by 5.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=79)** The output is the same.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=82)** Here are the C language assignment operators.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=85)** Each allows you to self-modify a variable by the arithmetic operator shown.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=91)** The secret to remembering the order is that the equal sign goes last.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=95)** For example, this expression subtracts 5 from the value of variable a, but this expression assigns negative 5 to the value of variable a.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-assignment-operators?u=76281980&t=105)** The equal sign goes last.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** self (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Working with relational operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Relational operators are used in C to compare two values or expressions.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=5)** They should be familiar to you, less than, greater than, less than or equal to, greater than or equal to, and also, is equal to and not equal to.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=17)** These operators must go in this order, with the equal sign coming last.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=22)** The is equal to operator is used for comparison, which is why I call it "Is equal to," and not "Equal."
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=28)** The single equal sign is the assignment operator and there's a big difference between the two.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=35)** Finally, not equal.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=36)** The exclamation point in programming reads, "Not."
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=41)** These operators dance with a keyword, such as if, while, and for, that uses the comparison to alter program flow.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-relational-operators?u=76281980&t=49)** The expression evaluates either true or false to control code flow.

#### Using logical operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C languages' two primary logical operators are AND and OR.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=5)** The logical and operator is two ampersands.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=8)** For a logical AND expression to be true, both of its conditions must be true.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=14)** The logical OR operator is two pipes or vertical bars.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=19)** For a logical OR operation to be true, either of its conditions must be true.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=26)** Typically, the logical expressions are used with relational operators as shown in this exercise file.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=32)** At line nine, if the value of variable A is greater than 6 and its value is less than 15, this expression evaluates true, because both conditions are true.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=44)** As the value of variable A loops from 1 to 20, each value is processed in this manner.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=53)** In the output, you see asterisks prefixing the output from lines 7 through 14.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=61)** Now edit the logical expression so that it reads: When ( a < 6 || a > 15 )
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=75)** Can you guess what the output might be?
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=83)** Only values less than 6 and greater than 15 have asterisks.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=92)** Edit the expression again.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=93)** Let's replace it with ( a >= 16 || a == 5 )
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=108)** Remember, "is equal to".
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=116)** Five has an asterisk, as do values 16 and greater.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=122)** Internally, the compiler uses 0 as the value for false.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=126)** Nonzero values are true.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=128)** Be aware that in some programming languages,
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=130)** - 1 is considered false.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=133)** It's a true value in C because all nonzero values are true.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=140)** When you goof up and use the assignment operator, a single equal sign, instead of the "is equal to" operator, two equal signs, the compiler evaluates the expression based on the result of the assignment.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=151)** Assigning a nonzero value to a variable is true.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=155)** Assigning 0 to variable A here would be interpreted as false.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=159)** Many compilers issue a warning when you attempt to use an assignment operator as a comparison.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=165)** Remember that two equal signs means "is equal to".
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=170)** When you pack more than two logical operators into an expression, they're evaluated left to right as you read them.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=176)** I will edit this "if" decision to be a little bit more complicated.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=181)** When ( a <= 5 || a >= 16 || a == 10).
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=200)** Here, all values less than or equal to 5, or greater than or equal to 16, or the value 10 are prefixed with asterisks.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=210)** Any of these conditions can be true.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=217)** Here is the output.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=221)** It may be difficult to wrap your head around some of these complex constructions.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=226)** Remember, evaluation goes left to right as you read it.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-logical-operators?u=76281980&t=230)** Apply parentheses around any expressions you want to evaluate first, otherwise you can tie yourself into a knot of trouble.

> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-bitwise-operators?u=76281980&t=7)** The four covered in this video are Bitwise AND:&, Bitwise Inclusive OR: |, Bitwise Exclusive OR: ^, and the One's compliment:~.

#### Exploring unary operators

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C Language unary operators affect only one variable.
>

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=8)** Here's the full list, including the sizeof operator, which is both an operator and a C language keyword.

>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=21)** It returns a size_t value, which is an unsigned positive integer, representing the number of bytes of memory the item occupies.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=29)** You must include the stdio.h header file to use the size_t typedef in your code, the operator's used most often when allocating memory or dealing with pointers.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=42)** In this code, six variables are declared, the four standards, char and float and double.

>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=59)** Within the printf statement, the size of operator is applied to each variable or data type.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-unary-operators?u=76281980&t=71)** The values shown here might not be the same on every system, but you see how the sizeof operator works to return the number of bytes occupied by each variable or storage space.

#### Using the ternary operator
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-the-ternary-operator?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=8)** This construction uses the C language keywords, switch, case, and default to form what's called a selection statement.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=17)** You also see the break keyword associated with this structure.
>

>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=28)** Unlike if the switch statement doesn't evaluate a condition.

> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=58)** If a match is made, the statement's belonging to the case statement are executed, otherwise they're skipped, and the next case statement is evaluated.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=68)** Program flow is altered by the break keyword which jumps execution down to the statement following the switch case structures block, the final curly bracket.

>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=84)** The switch statement uses the value input, variable A, as its expression.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-the-switch-case-structure?u=76281980&t=89)** When the value is one at line 12, the put statement executes and the break keyword exits the block.

#### Creating a for loop
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another way to control program flow is one of the basic concepts of programming, the loop.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=7)** A loop is one or more statements that repeat.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=9)** The loop's condition determines how many times the statements repeat.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=13)** Three C language keywords deal with loops.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=16)** For, while, and do.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=21)** Loops feature three parts, a starting condition or initialization, an expression that must remain true for the statements to repeat, and an exit condition to terminate the loop.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=34)** In a for loop, all three conditions are expressed in the keywords parentheses shown in this exercise file.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=41)** Two semicolons, separate the three expressions in a for statement.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=45)** The starting condition or loop initialization appears first, variable a is initialized to zero.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=52)** Next comes the condition.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=54)** When true, the loop repeats.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=56)** Here, the loop repeats as long as the value of variable a is less than 10.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=62)** Finally, comes an expression that's executed each time the loop repeats.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=66)** Variable a is incremented.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=68)** This expression eventually triggers the loop's termination.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=72)** This construction is complex with lots of symbols and whatnot still in a for loop.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-for-loop?u=76281980&t=78)** All of the loop's parts appear in one spot.
>

#### Setting up a while loop
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C language keyword while constructs a loop of one or more repeating statements.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=5)** The keyword is followed by parentheses containing the loop's expression.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=9)** While this expression evaluates true, the statements repeat.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=13)** Like other C constructions when more than a single statement is repeated, they all must be enclosed in braces.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=20)** Unlike a for loop, the while loop statement contains only the while true looping condition, which must remain true for the statements to repeat.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=29)** Therefore, the loop's condition must be initialized before the loop starts as a separate statement.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/setting-up-a-while-loop?u=76281980&t=35)** Further, the loop's statements must contain its exit strategy, something that alters the condition the while statement evaluates and eventually terminates the loop.
>

#### Nesting loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's really nothing a for loop can do that a while loop cannot do.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=3)** Though there's one thing you can do better with a for loop than a while loop, and that's nesting loops.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=10)** A nested loop is one loop inside another, as this exercise file demonstrates.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=14)** The outer loop uses variable row to count from one through 10.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=19)** The inner loop uses variable column to count from value letters A through J.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=24)** The printf statement outputs the current row and column values plus three spaces.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=29)** And a putchar statement at line 14 adds a new line character to the end of each line output, making a nice, neat grid.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=40)** Run to see the nice, neat grid.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=44)** And there it is.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=47)** Nested loops are most frequently related to information shown in grids.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=51)** The outer loop repeats only after the inner loop is done repeating a given number of times.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=59)** This exercise file shows the while loop version of the same code, because the while loop statement contains only the looping condition for additional statements that are required to make these nested loops work.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=72)** Variable row is initialized at line eight before its outer while loop.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=77)** Variable column is initialized at line 11 outside its inner loop, and both variables are manipulated within their loops to ensure that the exit condition is met.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=93)** Run the code to see the output, which is identical to the for loop example.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=100)** Because the while loop version of this code has four more statements and in this format it uses four more lines, most coders prefer nested for loops instead of nested while loops.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/nesting-loops?u=76281980&t=111)** The for loop format just makes it easier to follow, but otherwise there's no difference in output or performance.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Exercise Files:** exercise file (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Breaking out of a loop
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A loop repeats its statements until the terminating condition is met or until a break statement is encountered.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=7)** In this code, input from the user is requested.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=10)** The loop repeats based on the value input or until the value of variable A reaches nine, run.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=21)** I'm going to try a huge value like 300, but you see the loop only repeats 10 times.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=30)** The IF statement at line 12 tests to see when the value of variable A is equal to nine.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=35)** If so, its sole statement break is executed, the loop is broken, and execution continues outside the loops block.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=46)** When used in a nested loop break halts only the loop it sits in here, you see nested for loops.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=54)** At line 13 an if test is made, if variable column is equal to the letter E, the inner loop is broken run, and the table stops output at all rows after letter E, only the inner loop is affected.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=76)** You can use break to avoid entangling your code in an endless loop.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=81)** Here, the while loops condition is one, true.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=84)** The loop has no exit condition except for the IF test at line 11.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=90)** If the value of variable CH is equal to the exclamation point, the loop breaks.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=100)** Type a line of text, exclamation point.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=105)** As you type the characters are red, but they're not really processed until you press the enter key and the input string is flushed, meaning the exclamation point is red and the program stops.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=118)** This code is one example of a useful endless loop done on purpose.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=123)** The for version of this loop is, I pronounce this construction as forever.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=131)** The semicolons are required, but their expressions are optional.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=135)** The code works the same as the endless while loop example.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=141)** The continue keyword can be considered the opposite of the break keyword, though it's used less frequently, it directs the loop to spin again skipping over the remaining statements in the loops block.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=152)** In this code, when the value of variable A is less than 10, the loop just repeats otherwise variable A is incremented again at line 14.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=162)** The exit condition for this loop is presented at line 15.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=171)** You see that for values greater than 10, the number output increases by two.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=178)** The reason for the increase is that the second incrementing statement is skipped over originally, thanks to the continued statement, but once the value of A is 10 or greater, the remaining statements are executed.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/breaking-out-of-a-loop?u=76281980&t=190)** This is one example of how a continue statement can be used within a loop.

#### Avoiding the goto keyword
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The GOTO keyword alters program flow, but it does so in a manner that's difficult to follow.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=6)** Therefore, using it isn't advised, nor is it even necessary.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=10)** This exercise file shows the GOTO construction.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=12)** The keyword at line 11 is followed by a label which indicates where execution jumps.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=18)** The label is located at line seven at the start of the line.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=22)** The label name is followed by a colon, not a semicolon.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=26)** This is the label format in C with a label name using the same naming convention as for a variable.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=32)** The GOTO jump works only within a function.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=35)** So both the GOTO statement and its label appear here in the main function.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=40)** Effectively, this code shows a loop counting down from 10 to one, and there's the output.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=53)** Better ways are available to generate this same type of output without using a GOTO statement.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=58)** Further, you can't use a break keyword to disrupt such a loop because a loop isn't truly implemented here, there's nothing to break.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=68)** Here you see one way to break out of a GOTO construction.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=72)** Yes, what's required is another GOTO statement and another label.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=76)** The code loops down from 10 to four, which can be better handled with a for while loop and the break statement.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=83)** I hope you can see how messy things can get with a GOTO statement.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/avoiding-the-goto-keyword?u=76281980&t=87)** This is one reason why my advice is just to forget about using GoTo in your code.

> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-interpreting-commands?u=76281980&t=216)** I type a question mark there. That's much better.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-interpreting-commands?u=76281980&t=219)** This is how you do it.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-interpreting-commands?u=76281980&t=220)** I hope that your solution met with success.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-interpreting-commands?u=76281980&t=223)** In fact, if you're up to it, you can continue coding the program to actually do something interesting and fun.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-interpreting-commands?u=76281980&t=229)** That task you can complete on your own.
>

#### Creating a function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] C offers no rules regarding when functions are necessary or required.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=4)** It's best, however, to write a function when you use the same statements or perform the same process repeatedly in the code, for example, write a function for an input prompt used several times.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=16)** You can also build a function to handle a specific task, for example, a complex calculation.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=21)** Doing so makes your code more readable.

>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=27)** Again, there are no rules.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=30)** When you code a function, you set its data type for the value returned, or you use void when no value is returned.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=37)** Then comes the function name.
>

>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=46)** Functions with no arguments list void in the parentheses.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=51)** A set of curly braces encloses the functions statements.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=56)** Even when the function has only one statement, enclose it in braces.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=61)** Functions that return a value must have a return statement to pass the value back to the caller.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=68)** One more thing, functions must be prototyped, which introduces the function to the compiler.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=74)** This way, the compiler confirms that the function is being used properly.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=79)** You have two choices, write the function first in the source code before the function is called elsewhere, or write a prototype statement at the top of the source code file, which repeats the function's declaration terminated with a semicolon.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=95)** In this code, the sum function appears after the main function.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=99)** It's used in the main function at line 8.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=103)** At line 3, the function is prototyped to introduce it to the compiler.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=107)** The prototype statement repeats the function's declaration, but it's a statement, so it ends with a semicolon.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=114)** By prototyping the function here, the compiler won't complain when the function is used in the main function at line 8.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=121)** To prove it, comment out the prototype.

>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=179)** I've cut and pasted the function so that it appears above the main function.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-a-function?u=76281980&t=184)** The program is going to run because the function appears first along with its definition before it's called in the main function.

#### Solution: Writing a function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here is one way to create the line function and I'll code it before the main function, which avoids setting a prototype.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=16)** The function returns no values and it accepts no arguments.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=19)** It's a void, void type of function.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=22)** Braces.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=24)** In the function, I need a loop to repeat 40 times for the hyphens.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=28)** Integer variable A handles this task.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=33)** And now the for loop.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=40)** Within the loop, output a single character, the hyphen, and after the loop, output a new line to clean up the display.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=52)** Don't forget the closing brace if your editor didn't add it automatically, and I'm going to stick a space in there just because it makes things more readable.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=60)** Run to see the output.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=65)** And there are the two lines generated by the line function.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-writing-a-function?u=76281980&t=69)** This isn't the only way to code a solution.
>

>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=3)** The value specified must match the function's data type.

> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=60)** The parentheses here are optional, unless the expression you're returning requires them.

>

> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=89)** Here you see the greetings function.

>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=99)** When the comparison is true, a message is displayed and the return statement exits the function early.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=105)** Because the function is void, return has no arguments.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=110)** A second return statement isn't required at the end of the function.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=114)** Because the function type is void, the function's execution stops after the final statement and control returns to the calling statement.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/returning-a-value-from-a-function?u=76281980&t=125)** And of course, unless it's evening, you see the default message.

#### Solution: Passing values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] For my solution, the line function requires two arguments, as seen by the function call here, all coded appropriately.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980&t=16)** The function returns no value, so it's void.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980&t=19)** It needs an integer value, which I've named length and a character value, C.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980&t=27)** The function needs a loop to repeat length times.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-passing-values?u=76281980&t=29)** Now, I could create another variable to use as the loop's condition, but instead, I'll just write the for loop like this.
>

#### Retaining values in a function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Variables used in a function are discarded when the function exits.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=4)** You can return the variable's value from the function, but when your code relies upon values remaining in the function, you can get into trouble.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=13)** In this code, both the main and funct functions use integer variable A.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=17)** It's not the same variable as its private to each function.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=24)** Run the code and you see the variable retains its value in the main function and in funct, the value isn't changed because again, they're separate variables.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=37)** In this code, I desire to have the value of variable A in the funct function be retained.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=43)** It's declared and initialized to zero here.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=46)** At line seven, its value is increased by 16.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=49)** The value is output then returned to the main function.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=55)** In the main function, the value is also output.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=57)** The funct function is called three times.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=60)** My hope is that each time, the value of variable A in the funct function is increased by 16.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=71)** And it doesn't work.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=72)** The reason it doesn't work is because the value of variable A is discarded when the funct function quits.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=80)** To remedy the situation, change the variable's storage class specifier from auto, which is the default, and it's not written here, change it to static.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=92)** The value of variable A is now retained.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=94)** It's not reset to zero when the function is called a second time.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=99)** That's because zero is assigned when the variable is declared, it's initialized.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=104)** This statement doesn't run again because static variables already exist when the function restarts.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=111)** Run the update.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=114)** Now you see that variable A's value is retained in the function and each call, it increases by 16.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=121)** So say that the code has instead this statement, static variable A, and then it's initialized later.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=129)** In this condition, the static variable is reset to zero each time the function is called.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=137)** So ensure that you initialize a static variable as it's declared or it might not work as you like.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/retaining-values-in-a-function?u=76281980&t=144)** The static classifier is necessary when using buffers or other storage within a function where the contents are required outside the function.

#### Creating recursive functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A recursive function is one that calls itself.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=3)** It's like a picture within the same picture or a door that opens into a room with a door that opens into the same room.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=10)** Yes, this concept is mind-boggling, but recursion has useful purposes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=16)** In this code, function recurse calls itself.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=20)** The problem with recursion, however, is that the function keeps calling itself with no end possible.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=27)** This condition is a serious concern with recursion.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=30)** Like a loop, you need a terminating condition for the recursive function.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=34)** Otherwise, all sorts of bad things can happen, primarily,  the program crashes.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=41)** Let's run to find out.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=48)** Oops.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=49)** In this configuration, the function has called itself recursively 261,939 times, and a really bad error has occurred.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=59)** This code is not a successful example of recursion.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=63)** But it can be fixed.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=66)** A successful recursion function has an exit condition, where the function returns to itself.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=72)** (computer keys clicking) The if statement provides the exit condition.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=78)** When the value of variable a is greater than 10, return.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=83)** Now let's check the output.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=89)** And you see the function recurses 10 times.
>

>

>

>

>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=137)** Then when it's done, the indents are removed.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=140)** Winding up and winding down.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=144)** This recursive function is unusual in that it calls itself within its own return statement at line seven.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=152)** The user is prompted for a positive value, and you see the statements here to validate input.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=157)** The factorial function is called at line 21.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=160)** Within the function, the factorial is calculated via the return statement, which multiplies the current value f with the value of variable f minus one.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=171)** The result is returned from the factorial function recursively.
>

> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=182)** I'll type a value such as five, where I know the factorial of five is 120.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=188)** This may be the weirdest thing you've seen, but it works.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=193)** Recursive functions have many useful purposes.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=196)** For example, a file utility can recursively scan subdirectories to look for files.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=201)** If you write a maze-solving program, the code for deciding which way to turn is often recursive.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=207)** The classic eight queens programming puzzle is solved most effectively by using recursion, and many other examples crop up, especially in mathematics, with continued fractions.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-recursive-functions?u=76281980&t=218)** Remember that your recursive function must unwind, and you'll be fine.

#### Chapter challenge: Write the functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-write-the-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-write-the-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright music)

> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-write-the-functions?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Here's the setup for this chapter's challenge.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-write-the-functions?u=76281980&t=8)** You see a prompt for two values, input the values, then a series of functions appear which don't appear in the code.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-challenge-write-the-functions?u=76281980&t=15)** They're not written yet, add, subtract, multiply, and divide.

>
