#### Understanding arrays
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this code, you see five float variables, each of which holds a highscore value and is named appropriately, but isn't this type of declaration inefficient?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=9)** There must be a better way to represent these similar values.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=15)** In C, an array is a collection of variables, all of the same data type.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=20)** That's the short definition.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=22)** Specifically, an array is a variable.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=25)** Arrays are declared like any variable.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=27)** They have a data type and a name, but the name is followed by square brackets.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=33)** A value within the brackets specifies the number of items or elements within the array.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=40)** Here's an array variable declaration.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=42)** The data type is float, the name is highscore, and the array contains five elements, five float values, that are collectively referenced in this array.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=54)** This is a better way to store five highscore values as shown earlier.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=59)** Like any variable, you can initialize an array when it's declared.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=64)** To do so, follow the declaration with an assignment operator, equals, and a set of braces to hold the assigned values.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=72)** Each element's value is separated by a comma, though the final value lacks a trailing comma.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=79)** And when you assign values in this manner, you can omit the number of elements from the declaration.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=84)** The compiler figures out the value on its own.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=88)** Individual array elements are accessed in this format.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=92)** The value in brackets represents the element number.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=95)** The value can be expressed as a literal or a constant, and it can be a variable or any valid expression.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=100)** But most importantly, you must remember that the first element of an array is always zero.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=109)** The highscore array has five elements.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=112)** Its elements are numbered 0, 1, 2, 3, and 4.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=116)** Therefore, variable highscore 2 is the third element of the array.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=122)** This numbering scheme is something all programmers forget occasionally, even myself.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=129)** Also, remember that arrays in C are non-dynamic.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=132)** This limitation means that you cannot alter the number of elements in an array when the program runs.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=139)** The alternative is to use a pointer to allocate storage, which is an advanced concept covered elsewhere in this course.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=147)** Though you set the size of an array, the compiler doesn't provide any bounds-checking.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=152)** In other words, it's possible to reference array elements that don't exist.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=156)** This causes various problems in a program, none of which are good.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-arrays?u=76281980&t=160)** It's important that your code track an array's size to ensure that bad things don't happen.

#### Solution: Creating an array
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-creating-an-array?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-creating-an-array?u=76281980&t=0)** - Here's my solution to the challenge where I started with a basic C language skeleton provided.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-creating-an-array?u=76281980&t=10)** Declare the array.

#### Building a structure
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A structure is a container for multiple data types.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=3)** It can hold an int and a float, or three ints, or any combination mixed and matched in various quantities.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=10)** The different data types combined to describe a single item, like a record in a database.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=17)** The keyword struct defines a structure.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=20)** It's followed by the structure name and a set of braces or curly brackets.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=24)** Within the braces are the structure's members, a list of variable declarations.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=30)** To use the structure, you create a variable of the defined structure type.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=35)** The structure must already be defined before a structure variable can be set.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=39)** Still, it's common to both define a structure and declare a structure variable in the same statement.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=45)** I'll show you an example in just a moment.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=48)** A structure's data is accessed through its members, the individual values.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=52)** These members are referenced by using the structure variable name, a dot, and then the member's name.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=58)** Within your code, structure members work like individual variables.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=61)** They can be assigned values, sent to a function, used in an equation, and so on.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=67)** In this code, the pixel structure is defined at line five.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=71)** Keyword struct, the structure's name, pixel, then its members enclosed in braces, integer variables horz and vert, and character variable color.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=84)** These members are declared like any variable, terminated with a semicolon.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=88)** The entire structured declaration ends with a semicolon, shown on line nine.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=93)** This part just defines the structure.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=96)** At line 10, the center variable of the pixel structure type is declared.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=103)** Lines 12, 13, and 14 assign values to each structure member.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=107)** The format is structure variable name, center, followed by a dot, and then the member's name.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=113)** The remainder of the code outputs to the structure's contents.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=116)** The structure members are used here, just like any variable in the code.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=122)** Run to see the output, and there's the structure's data.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=130)** As I mentioned earlier, you can both define a structure and declare a structure variable in a single statement, so I can eliminate line 10 by combining it here.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=142)** This statement now defines the pixel structure and declares a variable, center.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=148)** If more variables are required, you can add them as well.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=154)** Each variable, top and bottom here, has its own structure with the data assigned as the structure's variable name, dot, and then member name.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=164)** Like other variables, you can also create a structure variable and assign its contents right away, which I can do here.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=171)** The format is to use an equal sign, a pair of curly braces, and then values to match up the data types.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=181)** Now these lines can be eliminated.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=184)** Remember that each value must match the data type.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=187)** So here you see two integers and then a single character.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=191)** The final item does not have a trailing comma.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=195)** The output is the same as shown earlier.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/building-a-structure?u=76281980&t=201)** Remember the format to reference the structure's variables is the structure variable name, then a dot, then the member name as assigned in the structure.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** just like (1)

#### Putting structures into an array
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Just like other data types in C, you can create an array of structures.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=5)** Each element in the array is its own structure with its own members, as shown in this code.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=11)** The pixel structure has three members, integers, horz, and vert, and character color.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=18)** The next statement creates a pixel structure array named box with four elements.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=24)** Then you see way too many statements that set data for each structure member in the array.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=30)** The variable is formatted with the array name first, box.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=33)** Next are the square brackets with the element number, zero for the first element in the array, then comes a dot and the structure member.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=42)** Finally comes the assignment.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=44)** For each member of the first element, box zero is used.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=48)** Then comes box one, box two, and box three for each of the four elements in the array.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=55)** A for loop outputs all the elements contents.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=58)** Run to see the exciting output, and there you go.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=68)** Because all these values are known, you can easily assign them when the array is declared.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=75)** This works like assigning array values for typical data types, but for a structure it involves a little bit more overhead.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=84)** Start with curly braces.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=87)** And then for each element, you have to have your own set of braces with the values inside.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=93)** So the first one is 100, 50, and then character R.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=99)** This is element zero.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=101)** Next element one.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=102)** (keyboard clacking) Remember, the last element does not have a comma, then you see the closing brace and semicolon.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=116)** I can now remove all the assignments.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=118)** (keyboard clacking) The code's output is the same as before.
>

> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/putting-structures-into-an-array?u=76281980&t=133)** If you guessed declare the variable at the same time the structure is defined, you're correct.

> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-structures-and-functions?u=76281980&t=108)** To fix the problem, you must declare the structure outside of any function.

#### Using single character I/O
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A basic form of input and output involves characters.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=3)** For the C language, characters come from the standard input device, usually the keyboard, and they're sent to the standard output device, the terminal window or display.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=13)** Here the "putchar" function sends five single character constants to standard output, one statement after the other.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=21)** The final "putchar" function sends a new line, which helps make the display readable.

>

> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=120)** You see both characters now.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=122)** This is how stream input works and you must be mindful of it as you code basic input output in C.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=132)** Finally, I want to address why the "getchar" function returns an integer and not a character value.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=138)** This exercise file is what I call a typewriter program.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=141)** It accepts standard input and then turns around and sends the same character to standard output, "getchar" and "putchar."
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=150)** At line 10, the character input is compared with the EOF, end of file, constant, which is an integer.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=157)** Run the code.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=159)** Now there's no prompt.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=161)** The program is running right now and I can type some text, (keyboard clicks) press enter, and the buffer is flushed and then the characters are output.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=170)** To end the program, you must press the "EOF" character, which in Unix is Control D, and the program is done.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=179)** If you run this under Windows, press Control Z, which is that operating system's end of file character.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=187)** What I'm trying to explain is why "getchar" and "putchar" work with integer values and not character values.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=193)** The EOF constant is an integer.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=197)** You cannot read this value if you use a character variable type.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-single-character-i-o?u=76281980&t=202)** This explains why "A," in this code and all variables you would use with "getchar" and "putchar" must be integers and not characters.

#### Testing and converting characters
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/testing-and-converting-characters?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/testing-and-converting-characters?u=76281980&t=5)** These are often called the C-type functions after the header file name, ctype.h.

> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/testing-and-converting-characters?u=76281980&t=11)** The C-type functions that test characters are often referred to as the is functions, because their names begin with the letters I and S.

>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=11)** I use an if else, if else decision tree to convert between upper and lowercase at line 12.

> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=24)** It uses two C type conversions is upper and two lower.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=30)** These statements don't convert the character in the string.

>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=43)** The is lower and two upper functions are used.

>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=48)** It does not alter the original string and then the else condition handles all other characters in the string as is.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-character-i-o-and-conversions?u=76281980&t=56)** Something that's easily forgotten in a while loop here at line 18.

#### Understanding strings in C
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The C language lacks a string data type.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=3)** Instead, a character array represents a string.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=6)** The final character in the array must be a null character.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=10)** It terminates the string.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=12)** This termination is what makes the character array a string and not just a collection of characters.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=18)** The null character is ASCII character code zero.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=21)** It's represented as the character literal \0.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=25)** This character isn't counted in the string's length, but storage must be allocated for the null character, which can trip you up if you're not careful.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=34)** The null character isn't the same as the null pointer constant.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=38)** The null character has nothing to do with pointers.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=41)** To declare a string in C, you create a character array.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=46)** This example is named string.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=48)** The square brackets are empty, and a string literal is assigned.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=52)** The compiler allocates the proper amount of storage for the string.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=57)** The null character is appended automatically to all string literals.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=62)** A more tedious way to allocate a string is to specify individual character values.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=68)** This construction works, but remember to add the null character, or what you've created is a character array and not a string.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=77)** When allocating string storage, for example, text the user inputs, you must set the size of the buffer to accommodate all the text plus the null character.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=88)** This buffer has room for 31 characters plus the null character, to make a total buffer size of 32 characters.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=95)** The string can be shorter, but the null character is always the last character in the string.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=101)** If you put more characters into a string buffer than are allocated, you get overflow, which is bad.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=108)** As a programmer, you must ensure that strings or any array never overflows.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=113)** Other programming languages are strict on this point, but not C.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=117)** It's vital that you monitor character input to ensure that buffers don't overflow.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=124)** The C library comes with mini functions to examine and manipulate strings.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=128)** These functions are prototyped in the string.h header file.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=132)** They perform basic actions such as returning a string's length, searching for text in a string, copying strings and so on.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=142)** Unlike other programming languages, you don't use operators to manipulate a string in C.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=147)** For example, you can't add two strings.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=150)** Instead, you use a function to stick one string on the end of another string.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=155)** For functions not available in the standard library, you write your own string functions.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-strings-in-c?u=76281980&t=160)** Just remember that the null character lurks at the end of the string, and you'll be fine.

#### Solution: Using printf placeholders
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The challenge calls for three variables, a character, an integer, and a float.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=10)** These are mine, and the names I've chosen.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=12)** I'm also assigning the values as the variables are declared, which saves quite a few statements.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=17)** The first print F statement outputs a summary.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=20)** You have purchased percent D, percent S at percent.2F each.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=27)** These are the placeholders that match the data type.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=30)** Percent D for decimal integer, the quantity.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=32)** Percent S for the string item, and percent.2F for the real number price with only two digits after the decimal.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=42)** You also see the dollar sign here.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=44)** This is not part of the print F formatting command.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=47)** It is simply to put a dollar sign in the output.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=51)** The second print F statement outputs the total cost, again percent.2F to format that price, that total as a dollar amount, so only two digits after the decimal.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=64)** The dollar sign again, is just for show.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-using-printf-placeholders?u=76281980&t=67)** It's just a character.

#### Manipulating strings
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Two popular string manipulation functions in the standard C library are string copy to copy strings and string cat to concatenate strings or append one string to the end of the other.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980&t=13)** These functions are defined in these string.h header file, which must be included in your source code.

>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980&t=25)** The second argument is the source string, which is copied to the first arguments buffer.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980&t=30)** It's vital that the destination buffer be large enough to accommodate the string copied into it.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/manipulating-strings?u=76281980&t=36)** Remember, this is how you copy strings in C.

#### Chapter solution: Reading commands
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=0)** (uplifting music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Narrator] For my solution, I'm going to need a constant and two variables.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=10)** Constant integer size sets the buffer size.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=14)** Integer X is used as an offset, buffer input is where text input is stored.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=21)** Within the while loop, I need to initialize the offset to zero.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=26)** This statement appears before the prompt, so the offset is initialized each time a command is input.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=32)** The first task is to fetch input.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=38)** Input is stored in the input buffer.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=40)** The maximum size is constant size, which includes the null character.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=44)** STDIN is the standard input device.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=47)** The next step is to convert the new line to a null character, which strips the new line from the string.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=53)** For this task, I'll use a while loop.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=57)** Loop until the character in the input buffer is the null character terminating the input string.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=64)** Brace.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=65)** Check for and convert the new line when it's found.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=73)** When the new line is found, it's converted to the null character, and the loop stops.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=77)** But as long as I'm looping through the string, I can use this same while loop to convert any character to uppercase.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=86)** And I also need to increment variable X to keep processing the string and eventually reach the loop's terminating condition.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=95)** The string has been massaged and converted to uppercase.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=98)** The next step is to output processing command with the command.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=101)** I'll edit this comment.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=107)** The final step is to compare the input string with the word quit.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=114)** The word quit can be all caps here as input is converted to all caps.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=118)** If the result of this string compare operation is true, it equals zero, the loop is broken.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=125)** Run the code to ensure that everything works.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=129)** There's the command prompt, and the text is converted into uppercase.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=137)** Last, to actually make the program do something, you need to do more coding as the only command recognized is quit.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=143)** But you're well on your way.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-reading-commands?u=76281980&t=145)** If your program works the same, congratulations, you've passed the challenge.

#### Understanding pointers

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To understand pointers, you must explore variables.

>

>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=55)** These two items, a variables size and memory and its address are related to the concept of pointers in the C language.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=65)** A pointer is a variable that holds a memory location.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=69)** This definition avoids using the description, a pointer points at an address, which is true, but that's confusing.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=76)** It's better to say that a pointer is a variable that holds a memory location.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=81)** Which memory location does a pointer reference?
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=84)** A pointer holds the address of another variable or buffer in the code because the pointer itself is a variable, it can be modified, changing the address in some way.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=94)** It can also change or manipulate the data at the address the pointer holds.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=101)** Pointers are declared like any variable.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=102)** They have a data type and a variable name.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=106)** The pointer's name is prefixed by an asterisk when it's declared.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=110)** This is the unary asterisk operator, not the multiplication operator.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=116)** Like all variables, a pointer must be initialized before it's used.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=119)** An uni initialized pointer leads to trouble.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=123)** To initialize a pointer, it's assigned the address of another variable in the code, one that matches the pointer's data type, int for int, char for char and so on.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=133)** The ampersand operator is used to fetch the variables address.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=136)** You saw this operator used earlier in the exercise file.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=139)** You may also recall it's used in the scanf function.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=144)** Pointers can be assigned to the address of a buffer or any allocated chunk of memory.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=150)** Once assigned an address, a pointer has two personalities, which is yet another source of confusion.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=156)** Used by itself, the pointer variable represents an address location in memory.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=161)** Used with the asterisk operator, the pointer represents the data stored at that location.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=166)** This is known as de-referencing the pointer.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=170)** Regardless of how it's used, the pointer variable is always declared by using the asterisk operator.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=178)** Pointers present a major hurdle in learning C, and many programmers do avoid them, which is wrong.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=186)** As with any programming concept, the best way to get a grip on pointers is to see sample code and to experiment on your own.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/understanding-pointers?u=76281980&t=194)** And if you forget, just repeat that a pointer is a variable that holds a memory location.

#### Doing pointer math
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A pointer is a variable that holds a memory location.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=3)** So what happens when you add 1 to its value?
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=7)** Here I'm referring to the pointer variable as an address without the asterisk operator.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=12)** So in this example in this exercise file, you see pointer pa initialized to the address of variable alpha at line 8.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=20)** Line 9 displays the address stored in pa, the address of variable alpha, and then line 10 displays that address plus 1.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=29)** Let's see what happens.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=31)** Doing my best hexadecimal math, I can see that these two values differ by four bytes, which is the size of an integer variable on this machine.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=43)** Adding 1 to the address stored in pa increased the address by four bytes.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=49)** By the way, at this point in line 10, the pointer references some unknown chunk of memory.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=54)** This is unsafe coding practice, but I did it to illustrate a point.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=60)** Here's a better example.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=61)** Array twos is defined and contains five elements.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=64)** Pointer pt is set to the base address of the array at line 8.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=69)** No ampersand is required in the assignment, which is due to the special relationship between pointers and arrays.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=75)** The addresses are then output in lines 9 and 10.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=80)** You see the same four byte difference, but this address is okay because this location is part of the twos array.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=90)** Here is similar code, but with the array and pointer defined as character data types.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=97)** Now adding 1 to the address increments it by a single bite, which is the size of a character in memory.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=106)** In this code, both the pointer and array are double data types.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=112)** Adding 1 to the pointer's address yields an increase of eight bytes, the size of a double data type in memory.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=120)** What you're seeing here is pointer math.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=123)** Incrementing a pointer's address adds the size of its data type to that address.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=131)** How large are the data types?
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=133)** In this exercise file I use the size of operator on the four standard C data types, char, int, float, and double.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=144)** According to the size of operator, each variable type uses one, four or eight bytes of memory.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=152)** When you do math with an address stored in a pointer, the address is manipulated in increments equal to the size of the pointer's data type.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=162)** A character pointer is incremented by a single bite, the size of one character, but a double pointer is incremented by eight bytes, the size of that data type as it's stored in memory.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=174)** This is how pointer math works, and it's how the address stored in a pointer variable is manipulated mathematically.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-pointer-math?u=76281980&t=181)** The applications of pointer math become apparent as you dive deeper into the concept of pointers in C.

>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=164)** The way this construction works is the address of the array and then offset zero, which is going to change in a second, I realize it's unnecessary here, but all that is contained in parentheses to be the address that the dereferencing operator, the asterisk refers to.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=181)** This pattern is repeated for the rest of the elements.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=186)** Run to check the output, and it's the same.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=191)** This format shows a similarity between pointers and array notation, and it must be formatted this way, which is why I put the plus zero here.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=200)** If you modify this statement, if you remove the parentheses, what's calculated is the value at address pt plus one, which you can see in the output.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=213)** So instead of seeing four for the second element, you see three, which is the value of the first element plus one.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=221)** Instead, you want the value of the address pt plus one, which is obtained by this format.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=228)** I realize this is a bit weird, but it's how it works.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=232)** It also shows you an interesting relationship between pointer notation and array notation.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=239)** Here's a review of how pointers and arrays relate to each other.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=242)** The base of the array is like a pointer.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=244)** Both must be of the same data type, and given these conditions, element number n of the array is equal to the address of the pointer plus n in this format.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=257)** To access elements using a pointer, first, initialize the pointer to the array, all pointers must be initialized.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=264)** Use the asterisk operator to access the element, increment the pointer variable to access the next element and repeat these steps as necessary to access the entire array.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=277)** The relationship between pointers and arrays is interesting, but understand that these are two separate things in C.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=284)** For example, unlike an array, a pointer's storage can be resized dynamically as the program runs.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=290)** A pointer is not an array and vice versa.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=293)** Finally, using array notation is not a shortcut to avoid using pointers.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/comparing-arrays-and-pointers?u=76281980&t=299)** If you're learning C, learn and understand how to use pointers.

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Here's a skeleton to start my solution with the basic parts of the program, but also a string, title, as shown here.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=12)** This is the string my program outputs.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=14)** In order to use a pointer, I'm going to need a pointer.

>

>

>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=34)** I prefer a while loop to test for the null character at the end of a string.

> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=41)** The D reference asterisk t is the value at the memory address or a character in the string.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=47)** The loop repeats as long as the value at address t isn't the null character.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=53)** Add your braces.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=56)** Output the character.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=57)** (keyboard keys clicking) The asterisk accesses the character, not the address t.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, increment the address to the next element or character in the array.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=68)** This is the end of the loop, and that's it.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=71)** Run to see the string output.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=75)** Pointers don't intimidate me.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=78)** In the challenge, I suggested that you not mess with the address stored in t.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=82)** If you don't want to do so, you need a second variable to act as an offset.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=87)** I'll use integer variable x.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=90)** Remember to initialize x before the loop starts, set it to 0.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=94)** The loop condition is now based on an offset instead of the value in the pointer, so I must add the offset here.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=103)** The address is calculated as t plus x, the asterisk fetches the value at that address, t plus x.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=111)** Here, variable t is unchanged and variable x will be used to march through the loop one character at a time.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=118)** The put care statement must also be updated.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=121)** Once again, x is added to t, and the character fetched at that address.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=127)** Variable t is unaffected here, but variable x must be manipulated in order to have it march through the address as an offset.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=137)** Run, and the output should be the same.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=139)** Pointers don't intimidate me.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=142)** Remember, you must often retain the address stored in a pointer.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=146)** To do so, use another variable such as x here to offset that reference.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=152)** This way, variable t isn't altered, and can be used again elsewhere in the code.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=157)** This is one approach as to how that can be done.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-output-a-string?u=76281980&t=161)** I hope you successfully passed the challenge.

#### Allocating storage
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When a program needs a chunk of memory on the fly, you allocate storage and create a buffer.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=6)** The new buffer's address is saved in a pointer such as buffer declared as a character pointer here.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=12)** The malloc function at line eight allocates memory.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=15)** It sets aside 128 characters of storage, saving the address in pointer buffer.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=22)** Immediately an if statement tests for an allocation error.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=26)** If so, an error message is output.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=28)** The exit function leaves the program at once.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=31)** It works similarly to the return statement, but it can be used anywhere in the code.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=36)** This function is prototyped in the STDlib.H header file included here.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=40)** Upon success, text is output and the buffer has been created.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=45)** The buffer's storage is released at line 15.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=48)** Another text update is output and the program quits.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=55)** You can't see the buffer, nor does the code use it, but it was successfully created and its memory released.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=63)** In C the malloc function allocates memory.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=66)** It's defined in the STD.lib.H header file, which must be included in your source code.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=73)** Its only argument is the number of bytes required.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=77)** Bytes are defined as a size_T data type in C, which is essentially a character.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=82)** The value returned is an address, a location where the buffer exists in memory, upon an error the null constant is returned.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=93)** The address, the malloc function returns is stored in a pointer, which is how your code accesses the buffer.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=99)** When the program is done using the buffer, its memory is released by using the free function.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=104)** This function is also defined in the STD.lib.H header file.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=108)** The free functions argument is the address stored in the pointer return from the malloc function.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=114)** The free function returns no value.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=118)** On line eight the malloc function's argument could just be 128, as that's the number of bytes required to store 128 characters.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=126)** Instead of coding 128, I multiply the amount of storage I need by the data type size.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=133)** In this example, it's the size of a character which matches the pointer's data type.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=138)** Multiplied by 128, the function is going to return either an address or the null pointer constant.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=145)** After allocating memory, you must immediately compare the result with the null constant as shown here.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=151)** If true, the memory allocation failed and appropriate action is taken, an error message is output and the program exits.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=159)** Line 15 frees the buffer, memory is freed when the program quits anyway, but freeing it with a free function is good programming practice.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=170)** The code in this exercise file allocates storage for 20 integer values at line 10, a test is made to confirm success.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=179)** The for loop at line 18 both fills each integer in the buffer with a value and outputs that value.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=187)** You see that memory was allocated and used.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=191)** It's important that the memory location return from malloc variable buffer here.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=196)** It's important that this be retained.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=198)** If you modify the address, say you increment it to access various elements, the free function would fail.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=205)** This is the reason why I use pointer math at lines 20 and 21, as opposed to incrementing the buffer pointer variable directly.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/allocating-storage?u=76281980&t=213)** It's many times when you're coding that you need to save that address, which is why this format is used instead of incrementing the buffer directly.

>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=3)** The first is to have a pointer member of a structure, and the second is to have the entire structure be a pointer.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=10)** In this code, the person structure has a pointer member, name.

>
