>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=59)** You see, the person structure has a pointer and variable you declared.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=64)** Variable you represents the address of a person structure, unassigned.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=69)** So the first step is to allocate storage for the structure.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=73)** The malloc function uses the size of a person structure to allocate one person structure sized chunk of memory, assigning its value to the structure pointer variable you.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=85)** Because this structure is a pointer, the structure pointer member operator is required when referencing structure members.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=93)** The operator is ->, as shown on line 20 in the fgets function, and again on line 23 in the scanf function.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=102)** Here, the ampersand is required because the member is an individual variable, it's only the structure that's a pointer, not the age member.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=110)** The allocated structure buffer is freed at line 29.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=118)** The program runs the same as the proceeding example, though the structure's storage is allocated as the program runs.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=126)** This exercise file combines elements from the two proceeding examples.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=130)** The structure variable is a pointer and one of its members is a pointer, both must be allocated.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=137)** Line 13 allocates the structure, after successful allocation, the pointer member is allocated at line 19.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=146)** Structure pointer notation is used here as the structure is allocated as a pointer.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=152)** Otherwise, the members are referenced like any other member.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=156)** The allocation must work in this order.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=158)** First, the structure, then the structure member.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=162)** Likewise, when the structures are freed, free the member buffer first, then the structure buffer.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=169)** The program runs the same as the two earlier examples.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-pointers-in-structures?u=76281980&t=177)** The key to remember with structures and pointers is that a structure allocated as a pointer uses the -> operator to reference a member, not the dot.

#### Solution: Allocating structures
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-allocating-structures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-allocating-structures?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Like any involved programming project, it helps to handle things one at a time.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-allocating-structures?u=76281980&t=10)** The code skeleton here already includes the stdlib.h header file, which is needed for the malloc function.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-allocating-structures?u=76281980&t=16)** So I'm going to start with a structure, which I will call data.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/solution-allocating-structures?u=76281980&t=22)** The structure contains in integer member v and character member c, and immediately I assign it to pointer array variable alpha, which holds 26 elements, 26 pointers to this structure.
>

#### Passing pointers to functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you pass a pointer to a function, you're passing an address either directly or as a memory location stored in a pointer.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980&t=7)** A function that accepts a pointer as input, accepts an address.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980&t=11)** In this exercise file, the doubler function consumes the address of an integer variable, an integer pointer referenced as alpha within the function.

> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980&t=76)** Passing pointers provides a method for returning multiple values from a function.

> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/passing-pointers-to-functions?u=76281980&t=87)** It returns no value.

#### Chapter solution: Processing input
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here's my definition for the get_input function.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=10)** It's a character pointer function as it returns a string.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=14)** The argument is an integer, which I call size in the function.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=18)** Within the function, I will need two variables.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=22)** Character pointer A serves as the input buffer.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=27)** In integer X is initialized to zero.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=30)** This variable will serve as an offset.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=32)** I need the offset so as not to disturb the address stored in variable A.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=37)** The first step is to allocate the storage, which is based on the size value passed.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=45)** If the allocation fails, the null pointer is returned, which is tested for.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=49)** An error message is output.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=51)** Then I use the exit function to terminate the program.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=54)** I could just return the null pointer here, which is how I would code such a function for my own use.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=59)** But remember, the null isn't tested for within the main function, so the program should just quit.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=66)** The next step is to read input.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=70)** The fgets statement stores the input into the buffer referenced by pointer A.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=75)** It uses the same size value passed which is why I didn't add one to its value when allocating the buffer.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=81)** Input comes from the standard input device.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=86)** To remove the new line, I use a while loop.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=88)** Remember, it's a requirement that all references to the string be made by using pointer notation.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=98)** The Y loop condition tests the character at offset X in the buffer against the null character.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=104)** The loop processes the entire string until this terminator is found.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=108)** The notation *(a+x) references a character at offset X in the buffer.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=114)** If the character is the new line, replace it with the null character then terminate the loop, otherwise increment the offset and repeat the loop.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=124)** The result is that the new line, if any, is replaced in the string with the null character.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=130)** The final step is to return the buffer's address.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=135)** Variable A need not be declared static.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=138)** A function can return a memory location without losing the value.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=143)** The final task, which you may have forgotten, is to free the allocated buffers.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=147)** This code must take place in the main function as here are where the pointers your name and your city are referenced.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=157)** This approach works because the address allocated and returned from the get_input function is stored in these locations.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=163)** The free function recognizes the allocated addresses and releases the memory before the program quits Run to test the code,
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=184)** the input is read and the new line is removed, which makes the output look more readable.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-processing-input?u=76281980&t=189)** I hope that your output looks similar and that you've passed the challenge.

#### Challenge: Writing and reading files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/challenge-writing-and-reading-files?u=76281980)

#### Working with raw data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The file versions of the standard CIO functions write text.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=4)** To read and write raw data, you use the fread and fwrite functions.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=9)** The good news is that the basic file opening and closing operations remain the same.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=14)** In this exercise file, 10 integers are written to the file integers.dat.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=20)** The file is open for writing at line 10.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=23)** Within the loop at line 19, The fwrite statement consumes four arguments.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=29)** The first is a pointer, the address of the data to write.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=32)** In this case, it's the address of variable A, which increases in value from 10 in increments of five.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=40)** The second argument is the size of the item written.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=43)** Here I use the size of operator on the int data type.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=47)** The third argument is the number of items to write, one in this case, a single integer.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=53)** The final argument is the file handle.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=56)** Run to create the file.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=59)** The program has no output, but a file is created.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=62)** It contains binary information, not the text representation of the values written.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=68)** In a terminal window, I can view the file's raw data, and there it is.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=75)** Each integer occupies four bytes.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=77)** The first one is 0A for the value 10.
>

>

> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-raw-data?u=76281980&t=100)** A pointer, in this case the address of variable A, to store the value read, the size of the data chunk to read, the number of chunks, and the file handle.

#### Using random file access
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When a file is open for reading or writing, the operating system uses a file position indicator to keep track of where data is written to or read from in a file.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=12)** Sequential file access reads from or writes to a file one byte after another, front to back.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=18)** The file position indicator keeps moving forward until the EOF is encountered or the FEOF function returns true for a given file handle.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=29)** With random file access, the file position indicator can be set to any location in the file.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=35)** This feature works best for files that contain uniform data, such as a file of integer values, structures, or similar consistent data.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=46)** The fseek function controls the file position indicator.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=49)** It's prototyped in the stdio.h header file.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=53)** It's three arguments are the file handle, or stream, a long integer offset, representing an offset within the file and a constant representing from which position in the file the offset is measured.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=68)** Three constants are used for the whence argument, SEEK_SET to move the file position indicator from the start of the file, SEEK_CUR to move the file position indicator from its current position, and SEEK_END to move the file position indicator from the end of the file.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=88)** The function returns 0 upon success, -1 otherwise.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=94)** This code writes a series of structures to a file presidents.dat.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=99)** The structures are stored in the presidents array, defined and declared at the start of the code.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=107)** The fwrite statement outputs all records at once, then the file is closed.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=112)** Run the program to create the data file.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=117)** The program shows no standard output.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=121)** This exercise file puts the fseek function to work.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=125)** It opens the presidents.dat data file just created.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=130)** The user is prompted to enter a record to read.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=133)** Input is validated with an if test here at line 26.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=138)** The value input variable X is decremented at line 32 to ensure that the values input are in the range of zero through nine, which matches the offsets and element numbers of the structures written to the file.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=153)** This fseek statement accesses the open file referenced by handle fp.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=159)** The offset is measured in chunks of the person structure multiplied by the value stored in variable X.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=167)** The SEEK_SET defined constant directs the function to measure the offset from the start of the file.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=174)** Run the code.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=177)** Record four should be James Madison, and it is.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=184)** Remember, moving the file position indicator is a random access control.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=189)** It works best when the data in the file is uniform.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/using-random-file-access?u=76281980&t=192)** The fseek function heavily hints at this consistency with its second argument, which measures the exact offset.

>

>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-path?u=76281980&t=59)** As with the get CWD function, the CHDIR function is prototyped in the unistd.h header file.

>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-path?u=76281980&t=81)** This function returns zero on success, negative one otherwise, but I don't need to test it on the parent directory shortcut as it would be valid for all directories except for the root run the program and now you see the parent directory to the directory shown earlier.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-path?u=76281980&t=99)** Note that when your program is done, it returns to the same directory in which it was started.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/exploring-the-path?u=76281980&t=103)** So the CHDIR function has effect only why the program runs not afterwards.

#### Reading a directory
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A directory is a special type of file, like a database.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=4)** Its contents reference files on the storage media as managed by the operating system.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=9)** Just as you can open a file, you can open a directory to examine its contents and obtain information about the files stored there.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=18)** To open a directory, you use the opendir function.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=22)** This function is prototyped in the dirent.h header file.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=26)** Its argument is a string representing the path to a directory to open.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=30)** The value returned is a dir pointer, which is like the file pointer used to reference open files.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=36)** Once a directory is opened, you use the read dir function to read its contents.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=42)** This function is prototyped in the dirent header file.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=46)** Its argument is the directory handle.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=48)** The dir address returned from the open dir function.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=51)** The read dir function returns the address of a dirent structure, which contains information about a file in the directory's database.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/reading-a-directory?u=76281980&t=59)** Members of the dirent structure can be examined to describe files stored in the directory.
>

> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=99)** It needs a variable, at least the way I wrote it, r here to store the return value from the unlinked function, confirming that the file was deleted.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=108)** An if test checks the return value with zero, indicating success, messages or output based on success or failure.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=115)** You could also code this function without integer variable r, which is what a lot of programmers choose to do.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=122)** I'll show you how it's done.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=123)** Just remove variable r and we can set the unlink function right inside the if statement.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=132)** This approach works.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=133)** After all, variable r isn't used anywhere in the code, so why waste the storage?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=139)** Run to test all the functions, the output, everything, and the file is created, data written, it's closed, it's open, the text appears here, the file is closed, and then it's deleted.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=156)** I hope you can appreciate how the text message output provided feedback.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=161)** In fact, if you see an error, you can better hunt down where it is by tracking the error messages.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-managing-files?u=76281980&t=167)** I hope you've passed the challenge.

#### Calling the operating system
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For those times that your program can't do everything or you see no point in reinventing the digital wheel, you can run another program from inside your own.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=9)** The first and most popular way to pass off control to another program is to use the system function shown here.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=16)** This function is prototyped in the stdlib header file up here.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=20)** At line 8, the system function sends the clear command to the operating system as its argument.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=26)** This command clears the terminal window screen.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=32)** The screen is cleared.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=36)** The system function returns control to your program when it's done, but your program remains ignorant of the other program's output.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=45)** When the other command's output is important to your code, use the popen function to obtain it.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=51)** This function works like the fopen function, but a process is opened, not a file.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=56)** You see file variable P declared on line 5.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=60)** It's used at line 9 to open a process, specifically, run the command line program here, the one that reports the operating system's name.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=70)** The process name appears where a file name argument would appear in the fopen function.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=75)** R is the same here, the file mode, which is for reading this process's output.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=82)** After opening, working with the process works just like working with a file.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=86)** The input stream is provided from the process's output, so the program you run shouldn't be interactive unless your code can handle it interactively.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=96)** The while loop at line 17 fetches characters generated by the process and displays each one until the EOF is encountered.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=106)** The pclose function down here at line 23, it closes the open process analogous to the fclose function for a file.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=115)** Run to see the output of the uname command.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=118)** The uname commands text, Linux, is sent to your program where it's processed, and here, it's just output.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=126)** I've used this function in my code many times to capture output from a utility, to store and analyze data, and other magical things.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/calling-the-operating-system?u=76281980&t=134)** And it's all possible, thanks to the popen function.

> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-advanced-math?u=76281980&t=178)** One degree is equal to 0.01745 radians, which is what you get when you take two pi and divide it by 360.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-advanced-math?u=76281980&t=188)** If your human user desires to input values in degrees, you must convert them to radians for the C language trigonometric functions to work.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-advanced-math?u=76281980&t=198)** Then you need to convert them back so that the human gets the answer in degrees.

> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/doing-advanced-math?u=76281980&t=203)** And as always, if you can't find a math function in the library, you can code your own.

#### Working with time functions

> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-time-functions?u=76281980&t=7)** The data type for tracking the time is time_t shown here at line six.

> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-time-functions?u=76281980&t=25)** The time function at line eight fetches the current time as the Unix epoch value and stores it in time_t variable now.

> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-time-functions?u=76281980&t=199)** There you'll find the full documentation for the tm structure.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/working-with-time-functions?u=76281980&t=204)** These members provide time tidbits that can help you construct a date or time string, as well as assist in all the fun that surrounds doing date and time calculations in C.

#### Creating random numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whether you need a value for a simple computer game or to use for testing a function, random numbers are part of computer programming.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=8)** To generate a random number, you use the rand function shown here at line 10.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=13)** It's prototyped in the stdLib H header file which is included here.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=17)** The rand function returns an integer value which is output 10 times in this code.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=23)** Run to view 10 random values.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=26)** And there they are.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=28)** Most programmers want the random numbers to be within a certain range.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=32)** To clip them, you can use the modulus operator.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=36)** I'll append the modulus operator here at the end of the rand statement.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=41)** The mod 100 part of the expression caps the value assigned to variable r in the range of zero to 99.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=50)** Run to view the clipped random numbers.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=53)** And there they are.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=55)** If you don't want the random numbers to include zero, you have to modify the equation.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=60)** I generally type + 1 here, though you could just put 101.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=64)** The goal is to keep the range from one through the value specified, which would be one through 100 here, which is why I use the + 1.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=75)** Though only 10 numbers are output, the range is between 1 and 100 now.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=81)** Run the program again.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=84)** And you see the same values.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=86)** That really doesn't seem random, does it?
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=90)** The random number generator on a computer is pseudorandom in nature, meaning that the computer itself can't really generate a truly random number.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=99)** To mix up the results, you use the srand function, which requires a seed value.

> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/creating-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=123)** This value is used in the srand function to seed the randomizer.

> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=30)** Next comes allocation.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=32)** It's based on the size of an integer times the grid size.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=36)** A test is conducted to confirm proper allocation.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=41)** The for loop populates the grid with random values based on the grid size.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=46)** Pointer notation is used.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=48)** The random value fetched from the rand function is clipped to values from zero through 99.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=54)** Next, the grid is quick sorted, buffer address, size of the grid, size of an integer, and the compare function.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=63)** Next comes the tricky part, outputting the grid.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=67)** Here you see I've constructed the nested for loops.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=70)** They plow through the rows and the columns, and remember that internally the grid is stored as a linear array.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=77)** All of these are offsets in reference.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=79)** The key to the output is a printf statement, which I will code right here.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=86)** Output each integer with a width of two characters and a space on either side.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=92)** Using pointer notation, we have the base of the grid plus the row value times variable X plus variable Y.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=101)** This is the traditional output by rows, not columns, and the code works.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=109)** And there you see the output going from smallest to largest across rows, but that wasn't the challenge, it was just to confirm that the code worked, the grid is populated with random values, but I want to sort by columns, not rows.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=123)** The change is a lot simpler than you might have thought.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=126)** In fact, writing the numbers down on paper and then counting how they lay out helps.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=131)** But what you eventually end up doing is this, replacing X with Y and Y with X, and that's it.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=145)** And there you see the modified output.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=147)** Because constants are used in this code, you can easily adjust the grid size by changing the constant values.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=157)** Say I want a grid of seven rows and 10 columns.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=163)** That's all the change I need to make.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=165)** Now I can run and the output is seven rows of 10 columns and sorted the way I'd like it to be sorted.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=174)** I hope you can appreciate the power of programming.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=177)** When your code works properly and you thought things through, you can make adjustments like this and the program magically adapts.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=185)** Well, it's not magic, it's programming, and your skills that make it happen.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-c-programming-foundations/chapter-solution-sorting-a-grid?u=76281980&t=190)** Now that you have the foundations, you're well on your way to becoming a full fledged C programmer.
